<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Rembrandt</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Dutch painter and printmaker (1606–1669)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Dutch artist. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Rembrandt_(disambiguation)" title="Rembrandt (disambiguation)">Rembrandt (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Rembrandt Harmenszoon van Rijn</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <small>Dutch: </small><span class="IPA" lang="nl-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Dutch" title="Help:IPA/Dutch">[ˈrɛmbrɑnt ˈɦɑrmə(n)ˌsoːɱ vɑn ˈrɛin]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandtvanrijn.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Rembrandtvanrijn.ogg" title="Rembrandtvanrijn.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; 15 July 1606<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BY_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-BY-1">[1]</a></sup> – 4 October 1669), usually simply known as <b>Rembrandt</b>, was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age_painter" title="Dutch Golden Age painter">Dutch Golden Age painter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Printmaker" title="Printmaker">printmaker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drawing" title="Drawing">draughtsman</a>. An innovative and prolific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Masters" title="Old Masters">master</a> in three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_medium" title="Art medium">media</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> he is generally considered one of the greatest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_artists" title="Visual artists">visual artists</a> in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_art" title="History of art">history of art</a> and the most important in Dutch art history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gombrich,_p._420_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gombrich,_p._420-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_masters" title="Dutch masters">Dutch masters</a> of the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Category:Works_by_Rembrandt" title="Category:Works by Rembrandt">Rembrandt's works</a> depict a wide range of style and subject matter, from <a href="/wiki/Portrait_painting" title="Portrait painting">portraits</a> and self-portraits to landscapes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genre_scenes" title="Genre scenes">genre scenes</a>, allegorical and historical scenes, biblical and mythological themes and animal studies. His contributions to art came in a period of great wealth and cultural achievement that historians call the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Dutch Golden Age</a>, when Dutch art (especially <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age_painting" title="Dutch Golden Age painting">Dutch painting</a>), whilst antithetical to the <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque style</a> that dominated Europe, was prolific and innovative. This era gave rise to important new genres. Like many artists of the Dutch Golden Age, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jan_Vermeer" title="Jan Vermeer">Jan Vermeer</a>, Rembrandt was an avid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_collector" title="Art collector">art collector</a> and <a href="/wiki/Art_dealer" title="Art dealer">dealer</a>.
</p><p>Rembrandt never went abroad, but was considerably influenced by the work of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_masters" title="Italian masters">Italian masters</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Netherlandish_art" title="Netherlandish art">Netherlandish artists</a> who had studied in Italy, like <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Lastman" title="Pieter Lastman">Pieter Lastman</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utrecht_Caravaggists" title="Utrecht Caravaggists">Utrecht Caravaggists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flemish_Baroque_painting" title="Flemish Baroque painting">Flemish Baroque</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Peter Paul Rubens</a>. After he achieved youthful success as a portrait painter, Rembrandt's later years were marked by personal tragedy and financial hardships. Yet his etchings and paintings were popular throughout his lifetime, his reputation as an artist remained high,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and for twenty years he taught many important Dutch painters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_203_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_203-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rembrandt's portraits of his contemporaries, self-portraits and illustrations of scenes from the Bible are regarded as his greatest creative triumphs. <a href="/wiki/Self-portraits_by_Rembrandt" title="Self-portraits by Rembrandt">His self-portraits</a> form an intimate autobiography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gombrich,_p._420_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gombrich,_p._420-4">[4]</a></sup> Rembrandt's foremost contribution in the history of printmaking was his transformation of the etching process from a relatively new reproductive technique into an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_form" title="Art form">art form</a>. His reputation as the greatest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etcher" title="Etcher">etcher</a> in the history of the medium was established in his lifetime. Few of <a href="/wiki/List_of_paintings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of paintings by Rembrandt">his paintings</a> left the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> while he lived, but his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_master_prints" title="Old master prints">prints</a> were circulated throughout Europe, and his wider reputation was initially based on them alone.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3827" data-file-width="3020" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Prodigal_Son_in_the_Brothel" title="The Prodigal Son in the Brothel">The Prodigal Son in the Brothel</a></i>, a self-portrait with Saskia, c. 1635</div></div></div>
<p>In his works, he exhibited knowledge of classical <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a>. A depiction of a biblical scene was informed by Rembrandt's knowledge of the specific text, his assimilation of classical composition, and his observations of <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Amsterdam" title="Jewish Amsterdam">Jewish population</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_203_204_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_203_204-7">[7]</a></sup> Because of his empathy for the human condition, he has been called "one of the great prophets of civilization".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_205_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_205-8">[8]</a></sup> The French sculptor <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Rodin" title="Auguste Rodin">Auguste Rodin</a> said, "Compare me with Rembrandt! What sacrilege! With Rembrandt, the colossus of Art! We should prostrate ourselves before Rembrandt and never compare anyone with him!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodin_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rodin-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Rembrandt<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[a]</a></sup> Harmenszoon van Rijn was born on 15 July, 1606 in <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BY_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-BY-1">[1]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a>, now the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. He was the ninth child born to Harmen Gerritszoon van Rijn and Neeltgen Willemsdochter van Zuijtbrouck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> His family was quite well-to-do; his father was a <a href="/wiki/Miller" title="Miller">miller</a> and his mother was a baker's daughter. Religion is a central theme in Rembrandt's works and the religiously fraught period in which he lived makes his faith a matter of interest. His mother was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, and his father belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Dutch Reformed Church</a>. While his work reveals deep Christian faith, there is no evidence that Rembrandt formally belonged to any church, though some scholars, such as <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Willem_van_Loon" title="Hendrik Willem van Loon">Hendrik Willem van Loon</a>, suggest he may have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">Mennonite</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> and five of his children were christened in Dutch Reformed churches in Amsterdam: four in the Oude Kerk (Old Church) and one, Titus, in the Zuiderkerk (Southern Church).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a boy, he attended <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> school. At the age of 13, he was enrolled at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Leiden" title="University of Leiden">University of Leiden</a>, although according to a contemporary he had a greater inclination towards painting; he was soon apprenticed to a Leiden <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_painter" title="History painter">history painter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_van_Swanenburg" title="Jacob van Swanenburg">Jacob van Swanenburg</a>, with whom he spent three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houbraken_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Houbraken-15">[14]</a></sup> After a brief but important apprenticeship of six months with the painter <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Lastman" title="Pieter Lastman">Pieter Lastman</a> in Amsterdam, Rembrandt stayed a few months with <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Pynas" title="Jacob Pynas">Jacob Pynas</a> and then started his own workshop, though Simon van Leeuwen claimed that <a href="/wiki/Joris_van_Schooten" title="Joris van Schooten">Joris van Schooten</a> taught Rembrandt in Leiden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Houbraken_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Houbraken-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Unlike many of his contemporaries who traveled to Italy as part of their artistic training, Rembrandt never left the Dutch Republic during his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh,_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7327" data-file-width="5797" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh,_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Saskia_van_Uylenburgh" title="Saskia van Uylenburgh">Saskia van Uylenburgh</a>, c. 1635</div></div></div>
<p>He opened a studio in Leiden in 1624 or 1625, which he shared with friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/Jan_Lievens" title="Jan Lievens">Jan Lievens</a>. In 1627, Rembrandt began to accept students, which included <a href="/wiki/Gerrit_Dou" title="Gerrit Dou">Gerrit Dou</a> in 1628.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1629, Rembrandt was discovered by the statesman <a href="/wiki/Constantijn_Huygens" title="Constantijn Huygens">Constantijn Huygens</a> (father of the Dutch mathematician and physicist <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>), who procured for Rembrandt important commissions from the court of The Hague. As a result of this connection, Prince <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederik_Hendrik" title="Frederik Hendrik">Frederik Hendrik</a> continued to purchase paintings from Rembrandt until 1646.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of 1631, Rembrandt moved to Amsterdam, a city rapidly expanding as the new business capital of the Netherlands. He began to practice as a professional portraitist for the first time, with great success. He initially stayed with an art dealer, <a href="/wiki/Hendrick_van_Uylenburgh" title="Hendrick van Uylenburgh">Hendrick van Uylenburgh</a>, and in 1634, married Hendrick's cousin, <a href="/wiki/Saskia_van_Uylenburgh" title="Saskia van Uylenburgh">Saskia van Uylenburgh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Saskia came from a good family: her father had been a lawyer and the <i>burgemeester</i> (mayor) of <a href="/wiki/Leeuwarden" title="Leeuwarden">Leeuwarden</a>. When Saskia, as the youngest daughter, became an orphan, she lived with an older sister in <a href="/wiki/Het_Bildt" title="Het Bildt">Het Bildt</a>. Rembrandt and Saskia were married in the local church of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Annaparochie" title="St. Annaparochie">St. Annaparochie</a> without the presence of Rembrandt's relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> In the same year, Rembrandt became a <a href="/wiki/Burgess_(title)" title="Burgess (title)">burgess</a> of Amsterdam and a member of the local guild of painters. He also acquired a number of students, among them <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Bol" title="Ferdinand Bol">Ferdinand Bol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Govert_Flinck" title="Govert Flinck">Govert Flinck</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bull-28_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-28-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1635, Rembrandt and Saskia moved into their own house, renting in fashionable Nieuwe Doelenstraat with a view on the Amstel river. In 1639 they moved to a prominent newly built house (now the <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_House_Museum" title="Rembrandt House Museum">Rembrandt House Museum</a>) in the upscale 'Breestraat' (eng.: 'Broadway'), today known as <a href="/wiki/Jodenbreestraat" title="Jodenbreestraat">Jodenbreestraat</a> in what was becoming the Jewish quarter; then a young upcoming neighborhood. The <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_loan" title="Mortgage loan">mortgage</a> to finance the 13,000 <a href="/wiki/Guilder" title="Guilder">guilder</a> purchase would be a primary cause for later financial difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bull-28_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-28-24">[23]</a></sup> Rembrandt should easily have been able to pay the house off with his large income, but it appears his spending always kept pace with his income, and he may have made some unsuccessful investments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> It was there that Rembrandt frequently sought his Jewish neighbors to model for his <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> Although they were by now affluent, the couple suffered several personal setbacks; their son Rumbartus died two months after his birth in 1635 and their daughter Cornelia died at just three weeks of age in 1638. In 1640, they had a second daughter, also named Cornelia, who died after living barely over a month. Only their fourth child, <a href="/wiki/Titus_van_Rijn" title="Titus van Rijn">Titus</a>, who was born in 1641, survived into adulthood. Saskia died in 1642 soon after Titus's birth, probably from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. Rembrandt's drawings of her on her sick and death bed are among his most moving works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slive-71_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slive-71-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_(Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5470" data-file-width="4602" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_(Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rembrandt's son <a href="/wiki/Titus_van_Rijn" title="Titus van Rijn">Titus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Titus_as_a_Monk" title="Titus as a Monk">as a monk</a>, 1660</div></div></div>
<p>During Saskia's illness, <a href="/wiki/Geertje_Dircx" title="Geertje Dircx">Geertje Dircx</a> was hired as Titus' caretaker and nurse and also became Rembrandt's lover. She would later charge Rembrandt with <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_promise" title="Breach of promise">breach of promise</a> (a euphemism for seduction under [breached] promise to marry) and was awarded alimony of 200 guilders a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bull-28_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-28-24">[23]</a></sup> Rembrandt worked to have her committed to an asylum or poorhouse (called a "bridewell") at <a href="/wiki/Gouda,_South_Holland" title="Gouda, South Holland">Gouda</a>, after learning she had pawned jewelry he had given her that once belonged to Saskia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 1640s Rembrandt began a relationship with the much younger <a href="/wiki/Hendrickje_Stoffels" title="Hendrickje Stoffels">Hendrickje Stoffels</a>, who had initially been his maid. In 1654 they had a daughter, Cornelia, bringing Hendrickje a summons from the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Reformed Church</a> to answer the charge "that she had committed the acts of a whore with Rembrandt the painter". She admitted this and was banned from receiving communion. Rembrandt was not summoned to appear for the Church council because he was not a member of the Reformed Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> The two were considered legally wed under common law, but Rembrandt had not married Hendrickje. Had he remarried he would have lost access to a trust set up for Titus in Saskia's will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slive-71_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Slive-71-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rembrandt lived beyond his means, buying art (including bidding up his own work), prints (often used in his paintings) and rarities, which probably caused a court arrangement (<a href="/wiki/Cessio_bonorum" title="Cessio bonorum">cessio bonorum</a>) to avoid his bankruptcy in 1656, by selling most of his paintings and large collection of antiquities, but was allowed to keep his tools. The sale list survives and gives a good insight into Rembrandt's collections, which, apart from <a href="/wiki/Old_Master" title="Old Master">Old Master</a> paintings and drawings, included busts of the Roman emperors, suits of Japanese armor among many objects from Asia, and collections of natural history and minerals. But the prices realized in the sales in 1657 and 1658 were disappointing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> Rembrandt was forced to sell his house and his printing-press and move to more modest accommodation on the <a href="/wiki/Rozengracht" title="Rozengracht">Rozengracht</a> in 1660.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> The authorities and his creditors were generally accommodating to him, except for the Amsterdam <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Painters%27_guild" title="Painters' guild">painters' guild</a>, which introduced a new rule that no one in Rembrandt's circumstances could trade as a painter. To get around this, Hendrickje and Titus set up a <a href="/wiki/Dummy_corporation" title="Dummy corporation">dummy corporation</a> as art dealers in 1660, with Rembrandt as an employee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rembrandt Memorial Marker Westerkerk Amsterdam</div></div></div>
<p>In 1661 Rembrandt (or rather the new business) was contracted to complete work for the newly built city hall, but only after Govert Flinck, the artist previously commissioned, died without beginning to paint. The resulting work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Conspiracy_of_Claudius_Civilis" title="The Conspiracy of Claudius Civilis">The Conspiracy of Claudius Civilis</a></i>, was rejected and returned to the painter; the surviving fragment is only a fraction of the whole work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> It was around this time that Rembrandt took on his last apprentice, <a href="/wiki/Aert_de_Gelder" title="Aert de Gelder">Aert de Gelder</a>. In 1662 he was still fulfilling major commissions for portraits and other works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> In 1662 one of Rembrandt's creditors went to the High Court (<a href="/wiki/Hof_van_Holland" title="Hof van Holland">Hof van Holland</a>) to contest that Titus had to be paid first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> Isaac van Hertsbeeck lost twice and had to pay the money he had already received to Titus, which he did in 1668.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_III_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Cosimo III de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Cosimo III de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany</a> came to Amsterdam in 1667, he visited Rembrandt at his house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rembrandt outlived both Hendrickje, who died in 1663, and Titus, who died in 1668, leaving a baby daughter. He died within a year of his son, on 4 October 1669 in Amsterdam, and was buried as a supposedly rich man as the heirs paid in burial taxes a substantial amount of money, <i>f</i> 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> but in a paupers grave in the <a href="/wiki/Westerkerk#Rembrandt" title="Westerkerk">Westerkerk</a>. It was in a numbered 'kerkgraf' (grave owned by the church) somewhere under a tombstone in the church. After twenty years, his remains were taken away and destroyed, as was customary.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_paintings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of paintings by Rembrandt">List of paintings by Rembrandt</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_etchings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of etchings by Rembrandt">List of etchings by Rembrandt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_drawings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of drawings by Rembrandt">List of drawings by Rembrandt</a></div>
<p>In a letter to Huygens, Rembrandt offered the only surviving explanation of what he sought to achieve through his art: <i>the greatest and most natural movement</i>, translated from <i>de meeste en de natuurlijkste beweegelijkheid</i>. The word "beweegelijkheid" is also argued to mean "emotion" or "motive". Whether this refers to objectives, material or otherwise, is open to interpretation; either way, critics have drawn particular attention to the way Rembrandt seamlessly melded the earthly and spiritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3731" data-file-width="3002" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rembrandt's only known <a href="/wiki/Seascape" title="Seascape">seascape</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Storm_on_the_Sea_of_Galilee" title="The Storm on the Sea of Galilee">The Storm on the Sea of Galilee</a></i>, 1633. The painting is still missing after the <a href="/wiki/Isabella_Stewart_Gardner_Museum_theft" title="Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum theft">robbery from the Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum</a> in 1990.</div></div></div>
<p>Earlier-20th-century connoisseurs claimed Rembrandt had produced well over 600 paintings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> nearly 400 <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etchings</a> and 2,000 drawings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rembrandtart_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rembrandtart-41">[40]</a></sup> More recent scholarship, from the 1960s to the present day (led by the <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_Research_Project" title="Rembrandt Research Project">Rembrandt Research Project</a>), often controversially, has winnowed his oeuvre to nearer 300 paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[b]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Old_master_print" title="Old master print">prints</a>, traditionally all called <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etchings</a>, although many are produced in whole or part by <a href="/wiki/Engraving" title="Engraving">engraving</a> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Drypoint" title="Drypoint">drypoint</a>, have a much more stable total of slightly under 300.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[c]</a></sup> It is likely Rembrandt made many more drawings in his lifetime than 2,000, but those extant are more rare than presumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[d]</a></sup> Two experts claim that the number of drawings whose autograph status can be regarded as effectively "certain" is no higher than about 75, although this is disputed. The list was to be unveiled at a scholarly meeting in February 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>At one time about ninety paintings were counted as <a href="/wiki/Self-portraits_by_Rembrandt" title="Self-portraits by Rembrandt">Rembrandt self-portraits</a>, but it is now known that he had his students copy his own self-portraits as part of their training. Modern scholarship has reduced the autograph count to over forty paintings, as well as a few drawings and thirty-one etchings, which include many of the most remarkable images of the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup> Some show him posing in quasi-historical fancy dress, or pulling faces at himself. His oil paintings trace the progress from an uncertain young man, through the dapper and very successful portrait-painter of the 1630s, to the troubled but massively powerful portraits of his old age. Together they give a remarkably clear picture of the man, his appearance and his psychological make-up, as revealed by his richly weathered face.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4781" data-file-width="3259" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg/170px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg/255px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg/340px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/A_Polish_Nobleman" title="A Polish Nobleman">A Polish Nobleman</a></i>, 1637</div></div></div>
<p>In his portraits and self-portraits, he angles the sitter's face in such a way that the ridge of the nose nearly always forms the line of demarcation between brightly illuminated and shadowy areas. A Rembrandt face is a face partially eclipsed; and the nose, bright and obvious, thrusting into the riddle of halftones, serves to focus the viewer's attention upon, and to dramatize, the division between a flood of light—an overwhelming clarity—and a brooding duskiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a number of biblical works, including <i>The Raising of the Cross</i>, <i>Joseph Telling His Dreams</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stoning_of_Saint_Stephen" title="The Stoning of Saint Stephen">The Stoning of Saint Stephen</a></i>, Rembrandt painted himself as a character in the crowd. Durham suggests that this was because the Bible was for Rembrandt "a kind of diary, an account of moments in his own life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the more prominent characteristics of Rembrandt's work are his use of <a href="/wiki/Chiaroscuro" title="Chiaroscuro">chiaroscuro</a>, the theatrical employment of light and shadow derived from <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a>, or, more likely, from the Dutch <a href="/wiki/Utrecht_Caravaggism" title="Utrecht Caravaggism">Caravaggisti</a>, but adapted for very personal means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup> Also notable are his dramatic and lively presentation of subjects, devoid of the rigid formality that his contemporaries often displayed, and a deeply felt compassion for mankind, irrespective of wealth and age. His immediate family—his wife Saskia, his son Titus and his common-law wife Hendrickje—often figured prominently in his paintings, many of which had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythical</a>, biblical or historical themes.
</p>
<h3><span id="Periods.2C_themes_and_styles"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Periods,_themes_and_styles">Periods, themes and styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Periods, themes and styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout his career Rembrandt took as his primary subjects the themes of portraiture, landscape and narrative painting. For the last, he was especially praised by his contemporaries, who extolled him as a masterly interpreter of biblical stories for his skill in representing emotions and attention to detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[48]</a></sup> Stylistically, his paintings progressed from the early "smooth" manner, characterized by fine technique in the portrayal of illusionistic form, to the late "rough" treatment of richly variegated paint surfaces, which allowed for an illusionism of form suggested by the tactile quality of the paint itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="23413" data-file-width="30000" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/The_Abduction_of_Europa_(Rembrandt)" title="The Abduction of Europa (Rembrandt)"><i>The Abduction of Europa</i></a>, 1632. Oil on panel. The work has been described as "...a shining example of the 'golden age' of <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> painting".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A parallel development may be seen in Rembrandt's skill as a printmaker. In the etchings of his maturity, particularly from the late 1640s onward, the freedom and breadth of his drawings and paintings found expression in the print medium as well. The works encompass a wide range of subject matter and technique, sometimes leaving large areas of white paper to suggest space, at other times employing complex webs of line to produce rich dark tones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was during Rembrandt's Leiden period (1625–1631) that Lastman's influence was most prominent. It is also likely that at this time Lievens had a strong impact on his work as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wetering-284_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wetering-284-57">[52]</a></sup> Paintings were rather small, but rich in details (for example, in costumes and jewelry). Religious and <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> themes were favored, as were <a href="/wiki/Tronie" title="Tronie">tronies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wetering-284_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wetering-284-57">[52]</a></sup> In 1626 Rembrandt produced his first etchings, the wide dissemination of which would largely account for his international fame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wetering-284_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wetering-284-57">[52]</a></sup> In 1629 he completed <i><a href="/wiki/Judas_Repentant,_Returning_the_Pieces_of_Silver" title="Judas Repentant, Returning the Pieces of Silver">Judas Repentant, Returning the Pieces of Silver</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artist_in_his_studio" title="Artist in his studio">The Artist in His Studio</a></i>, works that evidence his interest in the handling of light and variety of paint application, and constitute the first major progress in his development as a painter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4409" data-file-width="3293" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg/170px-Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg/255px-Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg/340px-Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical portrait from 1634, when Rembrandt was enjoying great commercial success</div></div></div>
<p>During his early years in Amsterdam (1632–1636), Rembrandt began to paint dramatic biblical and mythological scenes in high contrast and of large format (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Blinding_of_Samson" title="The Blinding of Samson">The Blinding of Samson</a></i>, 1636, <i><a href="/wiki/Belshazzar%27s_Feast_(Rembrandt)" title="Belshazzar's Feast (Rembrandt)">Belshazzar's Feast</a></i>, c. 1635 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dana%C3%AB_(Rembrandt)" title="Danaë (Rembrandt)">Danaë</a></i>, 1636 but reworked later), seeking to emulate the baroque style of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubens" title="Rubens">Rubens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup> With the occasional help of assistants in Uylenburgh's workshop, he painted numerous portrait commissions both small (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_de_Gheyn_III_(painting)" title="Jacob de Gheyn III (painting)"><i>Jacob de Gheyn III</i></a>) and large (<i>Portrait of the Shipbuilder Jan Rijcksen and his Wife</i>, 1633, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr._Nicolaes_Tulp" title="Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp">Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp</a></i>, 1632).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 1630s Rembrandt had produced a few paintings and many etchings of <a href="/wiki/Landscape_painting" title="Landscape painting">landscapes</a>. Often these landscapes highlighted natural drama, featuring uprooted trees and ominous skies (<i>Cottages before a Stormy Sky</i>, c. 1641; <i>The Three Trees</i>, 1643). From 1640 his work became less exuberant and more sober in tone, possibly reflecting personal tragedy. Biblical scenes were now derived more often from the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, as had been the case before. In 1642 he painted <i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i>, the most substantial of the important group portrait commissions which he received in this period, and through which he sought to find solutions to compositional and narrative problems that had been attempted in previous works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6387" data-file-width="5009" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Self Portrait</i>, 1658, <a href="/wiki/Frick_Collection" title="Frick Collection">Frick Collection</a>, a masterpiece of the final style, "the calmest and grandest of all his portraits"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In the decade following the <i>Night Watch</i>, Rembrandt's paintings varied greatly in size, subject, and style. The previous tendency to create dramatic effects primarily by strong contrasts of light and shadow gave way to the use of frontal lighting and larger and more saturated areas of color. Simultaneously, figures came to be placed parallel to the picture plane. These changes can be seen as a move toward a classical mode of composition and, considering the more expressive use of brushwork as well, may indicate a familiarity with Venetian art (<i>Susanna and the Elders</i>, 1637–47).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[58]</a></sup>
At the same time, there was a marked decrease in painted works in favor of etchings and drawings of landscapes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[59]</a></sup> In these graphic works natural drama eventually made way for quiet Dutch rural scenes.
</p><p>In the 1650s, Rembrandt's style changed again. Colors became richer and brush strokes more pronounced. With these changes, Rembrandt distanced himself from earlier work and current fashion, which increasingly inclined toward fine, detailed works. His use of light becomes more jagged and harsh, and shine becomes almost nonexistent. His singular approach to paint application may have been suggested in part by familiarity with the work of <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>, and could be seen in the context of the then current discussion of 'finish' and surface quality of paintings. Contemporary accounts sometimes remark disapprovingly of the coarseness of Rembrandt's brushwork, and the artist himself was said to have dissuaded visitors from looking too closely at his paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup> The tactile manipulation of paint may hearken to medieval procedures, when mimetic effects of rendering informed a painting's surface. The end result is a richly varied handling of paint, deeply layered and often apparently haphazard, which suggests form and space in both an illusory and highly individual manner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In later years biblical themes were still depicted often, but emphasis shifted from dramatic group scenes to intimate portrait-like figures (<i>James the Apostle</i>, 1661). In his last years, Rembrandt painted his most deeply reflective self-portraits (from 1652 to 1669 he painted fifteen), and several moving images of both men and women (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Bride" title="The Jewish Bride">The Jewish Bride</a></i>, c. 1666)—in love, in life, and before God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graphic_works">Graphic works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Graphic works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Christ_with_the_Sick_around_Him,_Receiving_Little_Children_(The_%27Hundred_Guilder_Print%27)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2876" data-file-width="4004" decoding="async" height="158" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Christ_with_the_Sick_around_Him%2C_Receiving_Little_Children_%28The_%27Hundred_Guilder_Print%27%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Christ_with_the_Sick_around_Him%2C_Receiving_Little_Children_%28The_%27Hundred_Guilder_Print%27%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Christ_with_the_Sick_around_Him%2C_Receiving_Little_Children_%28The_%27Hundred_Guilder_Print%27%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Christ_with_the_Sick_around_Him%2C_Receiving_Little_Children_%28The_%27Hundred_Guilder_Print%27%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Christ_with_the_Sick_around_Him,_Receiving_Little_Children_(The_%27Hundred_Guilder_Print%27)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Guilder_Print" title="Hundred Guilder Print">Hundred Guilder Print</a></i>, c. 1647–49, etching, <a href="/wiki/Drypoint" title="Drypoint">drypoint</a> and burin on Japan paper, <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Western_Art" title="National Museum of Western Art">National Museum of Western Art</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Rembrandt produced <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etchings</a> for most of his career, from 1626 to 1660, when he was forced to sell his printing-press and practically abandoned etching. Only the troubled year of 1649 produced no dated work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup> He took easily to etching and, though he also learned to use a <a href="/wiki/Burin_(engraving)" title="Burin (engraving)">burin</a> and partly <a href="/wiki/Engraving" title="Engraving">engraved</a> many plates, the freedom of etching technique was fundamental to his work. He was very closely involved in the whole process of printmaking, and must have printed at least early examples of his etchings himself. At first he used a style based on drawing, but soon moved to one based on painting, using a mass of lines and numerous bitings with the acid to achieve different strengths of line. Towards the end of the 1630s, he reacted against this manner and moved to a simpler style, with fewer bitings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup> He worked on the so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Hundred_Guilder_Print" title="Hundred Guilder Print">Hundred Guilder Print</a></i> in stages throughout the 1640s, and it was the "critical work in the middle of his career", from which his final etching style began to emerge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup>
Although the print only survives in two <a href="/wiki/State_(printmaking)" title="State (printmaking)">states</a>, the first very rare, evidence of much reworking can be seen underneath the final print and many drawings survive for elements of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2427" data-file-width="3471" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg/220px-Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg/330px-Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg/440px-Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Three Trees</i>, 1643, etching</div></div></div>
<p>In the mature works of the 1650s, Rembrandt was more ready to improvise on the plate and large prints typically survive in several states, up to eleven, often radically changed. He now used <a href="/wiki/Hatching" title="Hatching">hatching</a> to create his dark areas, which often take up much of the plate. He also experimented with the effects of printing on different kinds of paper, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_paper" title="Japanese paper">Japanese paper</a>, which he used frequently, and on <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a>. He began to use "<a href="/wiki/Surface_tone" title="Surface tone">surface tone</a>," leaving a thin film of ink on parts of the plate instead of wiping it completely clean to print each impression. He made more use of <a href="/wiki/Drypoint" title="Drypoint">drypoint</a>, exploiting, especially in landscapes, the rich fuzzy burr that this technique gives to the first few impressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>His prints have similar subjects to his paintings, although the twenty-seven self-portraits are relatively more common, and portraits of other people less so. There are forty-six landscapes, mostly small, which largely set the course for the graphic treatment of landscape until the end of the 19th century. One third of his etchings are of religious subjects, many treated with a homely simplicity, whilst others are his most monumental prints. A few erotic, or just obscene, compositions have no equivalent in his paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> He owned, until forced to sell it, a magnificent collection of prints by other artists, and many borrowings and influences in his work can be traced to artists as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Mantegna" title="Andrea Mantegna">Mantegna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hercules_Seghers" title="Hercules Seghers">Hercules Seghers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Benedetto_Castiglione" title="Giovanni Benedetto Castiglione">Giovanni Benedetto Castiglione</a>.
</p><p>Drawings by Rembrandt and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Rembrandt_pupils" title="List of Rembrandt pupils">his pupils/followers</a> have been extensively studied by many artists and scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[f]</a></sup> through the centuries. His original draughtsmanship has been described as an individualistic art style that was very similar to East Asian old masters, most notably Chinese masters:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mendelowitz_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mendelowitz-82">[76]</a></sup> a "combination of formal clarity and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphic</a> vitality in the movement of pen or brush that is closer to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese painting</a> in technique and feeling than to anything in European art before the twentieth century".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sullivan_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sullivan-83">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oriental_inspiration">Oriental inspiration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Oriental inspiration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt%27s_Mughal_drawings" title="Rembrandt's Mughal drawings">Rembrandt's Mughal drawings</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_208.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="932" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Rembrandt_208.jpg/170px-Rembrandt_208.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Rembrandt_208.jpg/255px-Rembrandt_208.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Rembrandt_208.jpg/340px-Rembrandt_208.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_208.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rembrandt drawing of an Indian Mughal painting</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="590" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg/170px-Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg/255px-Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg/340px-Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandtselfportraitweb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Role-playing in self-portrait as an oriental potentate with a <a href="/wiki/Kris" title="Kris">kris</a>/keris, a Javanese blade weapon from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company_in_Indonesia" title="Dutch East India Company in Indonesia">VOC era</a> (etching, c. 1634)</div></div></div>
<p>Rembrandt was interested in <a href="/wiki/Mughal_painting" title="Mughal painting">Mughal miniatures</a>, especially around the 1650s. He drew versions of some 23 Mughal paintings, and may have owned an album of them. These miniatures include paintings of <a href="/wiki/Shah_Jahan" title="Shah Jahan">Shah Jahan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dara_Shikoh" title="Dara Shikoh">Dara Shikoh</a>. They may also have influenced the costumes and other aspects of his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Night_Watch"><i>The Night Watch</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Night Watch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output
