<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_ronda_de_noche,_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="11528" data-file-width="14168" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/La_ronda_de_noche%2C_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg/220px-La_ronda_de_noche%2C_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/La_ronda_de_noche%2C_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg/330px-La_ronda_de_noche%2C_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/La_ronda_de_noche%2C_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg/440px-La_ronda_de_noche%2C_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_ronda_de_noche,_por_Rembrandt_van_Rijn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i> or <i>The Militia Company of Captain Frans Banning Cocq</i>, 1642. Oil on canvas; on display at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam" title="Rijksmuseum Amsterdam">Rijksmuseum</a>, Amsterdam</div></div></div>
<p>Rembrandt painted the large painting <i>The Militia Company of Captain Frans Banning Cocq</i> between 1640 and 1642. This picture was called <i>De Nachtwacht</i> by the Dutch and <i>The Night Watch</i> by Sir <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Reynolds" title="Joshua Reynolds">Joshua Reynolds</a> because by the 18th century the picture was so dimmed and defaced that it was almost indistinguishable, and it looked quite like a night scene. After it was cleaned, it was discovered to represent broad day—a party of <a href="/wiki/Musketeer" title="Musketeer">musketeers</a> stepping from a gloomy courtyard into the blinding sunlight.
</p><p>The piece was commissioned for the new hall of the <i><a href="/wiki/Kloveniersdoelen,_Amsterdam" title="Kloveniersdoelen, Amsterdam">Kloveniersdoelen</a></i>, the musketeer branch of the civic militia. Rembrandt departed from convention, which ordered that such genre pieces should be stately and formal, rather a line-up than an action scene. Instead he showed the militia readying themselves to embark on a mission (what kind of mission, an ordinary patrol or some special event, is a matter of debate).
</p><p>Contrary to what is often said, the work was hailed as a success from the beginning. Parts of the canvas were cut off (approximately 20% from the left hand side was removed) to make the painting fit its new position when it was moved to <a href="/wiki/Royal_Palace_of_Amsterdam" title="Royal Palace of Amsterdam">Amsterdam town hall</a> in 1715; the Rijksmuseum has a smaller copy of what is thought to be the full original composition; the four figures in the front are at the centre of the canvas. The painting is now in the <a href="/wiki/Rijksmuseum" title="Rijksmuseum">Rijksmuseum</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expert_assessments">Expert assessments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Expert assessments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rembrandt_catalog_raisonn%C3%A9,_1968" title="Rembrandt catalog raisonné, 1968">Rembrandt catalog raisonné, 1968</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter,_c.1655_(Frick_Collection).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5071" data-file-width="5832" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter,_c.1655_(Frick_Collection).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Polish_Rider" title="The Polish Rider">The Polish Rider</a></i> – Possibly a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lisowczycy" title="Lisowczycy">Lisowczyk</a> on horseback</div></div></div>
<p>In 1968 the Rembrandt Research Project began under the sponsorship of the Netherlands Organization for the Advancement of Scientific Research; it was initially expected to last a highly optimistic ten years. Art historians teamed up with experts from other fields to reassess the authenticity of works attributed to Rembrandt, using all methods available, including state-of-the-art technical diagnostics, and to compile a complete new <a href="/wiki/Catalogue_raisonn%C3%A9" title="Catalogue raisonné">catalogue raisonné</a> of his paintings. As a result of their findings, many paintings that were previously attributed to Rembrandt have been removed from their list, although others have been added back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup> Many of those removed are now thought to be the work of his students.
</p><p>One example of activity is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Polish_Rider" title="The Polish Rider">The Polish Rider</a></i>, in New York's <a href="/wiki/Frick_Collection" title="Frick Collection">Frick Collection</a>. Rembrandt's authorship had been questioned by at least one scholar, Alfred von Wurzbach, at the beginning of the twentieth century, but for many decades later most scholars, including the foremost authority writing in English, <a href="/wiki/Julius_S._Held" title="Julius S. Held">Julius S. Held</a>, agreed that it was indeed by the master. In the 1980s, however, Dr. Josua Bruyn of the Foundation Rembrandt Research Project cautiously and tentatively attributed the painting to one of Rembrandt's closest and most talented pupils, <a href="/wiki/Willem_Drost" title="Willem Drost">Willem Drost</a>, about whom little is known. But Bruyn's remained a minority opinion, the suggestion of Drost's authorship is now generally rejected, and the Frick itself never changed its own attribution, the label still reading "Rembrandt" and not "attributed to" or "school of". More recent opinion has shifted even more decisively in favor of the Frick, with <a href="/wiki/Simon_Schama" title="Simon Schama">Simon Schama</a> (in his 1999 book <i>Rembrandt's Eyes</i>) and the Rembrandt Project scholar Ernst van de Wetering (Melbourne Symposium, 1997) both arguing for attribution to the master. Those few scholars who still question Rembrandt's authorship feel that the execution is uneven, and favour different attributions for different parts of the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1598" data-file-width="1204" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg/170px-Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg/255px-Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg/340px-Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_with_the_Golden_Helmet" title="The Man with the Golden Helmet">The Man with the Golden Helmet</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie,_Berlin" title="Gemäldegalerie, Berlin">Gemäldegalerie, Berlin</a>, once one of the most famous "Rembrandt" portraits, is no longer attributed to the master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A similar issue was raised by Simon Schama in his book <i>Rembrandt's Eyes</i> concerning the verification of titles associated with the subject matter depicted in Rembrandt's works. For example, the exact subject being portrayed in <i><a href="/wiki/Aristotle_with_a_Bust_of_Homer" title="Aristotle with a Bust of Homer">Aristotle with a Bust of Homer</a></i> (recently retitled by curators at the Metropolitan Museum) has been directly challenged by Schama applying the scholarship of Paul Crenshaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup> Schama presents a substantial argument that it was the famous ancient Greek painter <a href="/wiki/Apelles" title="Apelles">Apelles</a> who is depicted in contemplation by Rembrandt and not Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another painting, <i>Pilate Washing His Hands</i>, is also of questionable attribution. Critical opinion of this picture has varied since 1905, when Wilhelm von Bode described it as "a somewhat abnormal work" by Rembrandt. Scholars have since dated the painting to the 1660s and assigned it to an anonymous pupil, possibly Aert de Gelder. The composition bears superficial resemblance to mature works by Rembrandt but lacks the master's command of illumination and modeling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>The attribution and re-attribution work is ongoing. In 2005 four oil paintings previously attributed to Rembrandt's students were reclassified as the work of Rembrandt himself: <i>Study of an Old Man in Profile</i> and <i>Study of an Old Man with a Beard</i> from a US private collection, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Study_of_a_Weeping_Woman" title="Study of a Weeping Woman">Study of a Weeping Woman</a></i>, owned by the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Institute_of_Arts" title="Detroit Institute of Arts">Detroit Institute of Arts</a>, and <i>Portrait of an Elderly Woman in a White Bonnet</i>, painted in 1640.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup> The <i>Old Man Sitting in a Chair</i> is a further example: in 2014, Professor <a href="/wiki/Ernst_van_de_Wetering" title="Ernst van de Wetering">Ernst van de Wetering</a> offered his view to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> that the demotion of the 1652 painting <i>Old Man Sitting in a Chair</i> "was a vast mistake...it is a most important painting. The painting needs to be seen in terms of Rembrandt's experimentation". This was highlighted much earlier by <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Konstam" title="Nigel Konstam">Nigel Konstam</a> who studied Rembrandt throughout his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rembrandt's own studio practice is a major factor in the difficulty of attribution, since, like many masters before him, he encouraged his students to copy his paintings, sometimes finishing or retouching them to be sold as originals, and sometimes selling them as authorized copies. Additionally, his style proved easy enough for his most talented students to emulate. Further complicating matters is the uneven quality of some of Rembrandt's own work, and his frequent stylistic evolutions and experiments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> As well, there were later imitations of his work, and restorations which so seriously damaged the original works that they are no longer recognizable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> It is highly likely that there will never be universal agreement as to what does and what does not constitute a genuine Rembrandt.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Painting_materials">Painting materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Painting materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1010" data-file-width="776" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Saskia as Flora</i>, 1635</div></div></div>
<p>Technical investigation of Rembrandt's paintings in the possession of the <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie_Alte_Meister" title="Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister">Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie_Alte_Meister_(Kassel)" title="Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister (Kassel)">Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister (Kassel)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> was conducted by Hermann Kühn in 1977. The pigment analyses of some thirty paintings have shown that Rembrandt's palette consisted of the following pigments: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead_white" title="Lead white">lead white</a>, various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ochres" title="Ochres">ochres</a>, Vandyke brown, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bone_Black_(pigment)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bone Black (pigment) (page does not exist)">bone black</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charcoal_black" title="Charcoal black">charcoal black</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamp_black" title="Lamp black">lamp black</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vermilion" title="Vermilion">vermilion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madder_lake" title="Madder lake">madder lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yellow_lake&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yellow lake (page does not exist)">yellow lake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lead-tin-yellow" title="Lead-tin-yellow">lead-tin-yellow</a>. One painting (Saskia van Uylenburgh as Flora)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> reportedly contains <a href="/wiki/Gamboge" title="Gamboge">gamboge</a>. Rembrandt very rarely used pure blue or green colors, the most pronounced exception being <a href="/wiki/Belshazzar%27s_Feast_(Rembrandt)" title="Belshazzar's Feast (Rembrandt)"><i>Belshazzar's Feast</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bomford_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-bomford-101">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Gallery_in_London" title="National Gallery in London">National Gallery in London</a>. The book by Bomford<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bomford_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-bomford-101">[95]</a></sup> describes more recent technical investigations and pigment analyses of Rembrandt's paintings predominantly in the National Gallery in London. The entire array of pigments employed by Rembrandt can be found at ColourLex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> The best source for technical information on Rembrandt's paintings on the web is the Rembrandt Database containing all works of Rembrandt with detailed investigative reports, infrared and radiography images and other scientific details.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_signature">Name and signature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Name and signature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_bue_squartato,_1655,_02.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG/170px-Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG/255px-Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG/340px-Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_bue_squartato,_1655,_02.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Slaughtered_Ox" title="Slaughtered Ox">Slaughtered Ox</a></i> (1655), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre" title="Musée du Louvre">Musée du Louvre</a>, Paris</div></div></div>
<p>"Rembrandt" is a modification of the spelling of the artist's first name that he introduced in 1633. "Harmenszoon" indicates that his father's name is Harmen. "van Rijn" indicates that his family lived near the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Roughly speaking, his earliest signatures (c. 1625) consisted of an initial "R", or the monogram "RH" (for Rembrant Harmenszoon), and starting in 1629, "RHL" (the "L" stood, presumably, for Leiden). In 1632, he used this monogram early in the year, then added his family name to it, "RHL-van Rijn", but replaced this form in that same year and began using his first name alone with its original spelling, "Rembrant". In 1633 he added a "d", and maintained this form consistently from then on, proving that this minor change had a meaning for him (whatever it might have been). This change is purely visual; it does not change the way his name is pronounced. Curiously enough, despite the large number of paintings and etchings signed with this modified first name, most of the documents that mentioned him during his lifetime retained the original "Rembrant" spelling. (Note: the rough chronology of signature forms above applies to the paintings, and to a lesser degree to the etchings; from 1632, presumably, there is only one etching signed "RHL-v. Rijn," the large-format "Raising of Lazarus," B 73).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> His practice of signing his work with his first name, later followed by <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>, was probably inspired by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> who, then as now, were referred to by their first names alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Workshop">Workshop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Workshop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Rembrandt ran a large workshop and had many pupils. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Rembrandt_pupils" title="List of Rembrandt pupils">list of Rembrandt pupils</a> from his period in Leiden as well as his time in Amsterdam is quite long, mostly because his influence on painters around him was so great that it is difficult to tell whether someone worked for him in his studio or just copied his style for patrons eager to acquire a Rembrandt. A partial list should include<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Bol" title="Ferdinand Bol">Ferdinand Bol</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Adriaen_Brouwer" title="Adriaen Brouwer">Adriaen Brouwer</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Gerrit_Dou" title="Gerrit Dou">Gerrit Dou</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Willem_Drost" title="Willem Drost">Willem Drost</a>,
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heiman_Dullaart" title="Heiman Dullaart">Heiman Dullaart</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Gerbrand_van_den_Eeckhout" title="Gerbrand van den Eeckhout">Gerbrand van den Eeckhout</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Carel_Fabritius" title="Carel Fabritius">Carel Fabritius</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Govert_Flinck" title="Govert Flinck">Govert Flinck</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Hendrick_Fromantiou" title="Hendrick Fromantiou">Hendrick Fromantiou</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Aert_de_Gelder" title="Aert de Gelder">Aert de Gelder</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Samuel_Dirksz_van_Hoogstraten" title="Samuel Dirksz van Hoogstraten">Samuel Dirksz van Hoogstraten</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Abraham_Janssens" title="Abraham Janssens">Abraham Janssens</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller" title="Godfrey Kneller">Godfrey Kneller</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Philip_de_Koninck" title="Philip de Koninck">Philip de Koninck</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Jacob_Levecq" title="Jacob Levecq">Jacob Levecq</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Nicolaes_Maes" title="Nicolaes Maes">Nicolaes Maes</a>,
<a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Ovens" title="Jürgen Ovens">Jürgen Ovens</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Christopher_Paudi%C3%9F" title="Christopher Paudiß">Christopher Paudiß</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Willem_de_Poorter" title="Willem de Poorter">Willem de Poorter</a>,
<a href="/wiki/Jan_Victors" title="Jan Victors">Jan Victors</a>, and
<a href="/wiki/Willem_van_der_Vliet" title="Willem van der Vliet">Willem van der Vliet</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Museum_collections">Museum collections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Museum collections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandts_house,_Amsterdam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg/170px-Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg/255px-Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg/340px-Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandts_house,_Amsterdam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_House_Museum" title="Rembrandt House Museum">Rembrandt House Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>The most notable collections of Rembrandt's work are at Amsterdam's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam" title="Rijksmuseum Amsterdam">Rijksmuseum</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Bride" title="The Jewish Bride">The Jewish Bride</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Mauritshuis" title="Mauritshuis">Mauritshuis</a> in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery" title="National Gallery">National Gallery</a> in London, <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie,_Berlin" title="Gemäldegalerie, Berlin">Gemäldegalerie</a> in Berlin, <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie_Alte_Meister" title="Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister">Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister</a> in Dresden, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Louvre" title="The Louvre">The Louvre</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalmuseum,_Stockholm" title="Nationalmuseum, Stockholm">Nationalmuseum, Stockholm</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schloss_Wilhelmsh%C3%B6he" title="Schloss Wilhelmshöhe">Schloss Wilhelmshöhe</a> in Kassel. The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Castle,_Warsaw" title="Royal Castle, Warsaw">Royal Castle</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> displays two paintings by Rembrandt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zamek_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-zamek-109">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Notable collections of Rembrandt's paintings in the United States are housed in the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frick_Collection" title="Frick Collection">Frick Collection</a> in New York City, the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Art" title="National Gallery of Art">National Gallery of Art</a> in Washington, D.C., <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Fine_Arts,_Boston" title="Museum of Fine Arts, Boston">Museum of Fine Arts</a> in Boston, and <a href="/wiki/J._Paul_Getty_Museum" title="J. Paul Getty Museum">J. Paul Getty Museum</a> in Los Angeles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_House_Museum" title="Rembrandt House Museum">Rembrandt House Museum</a> in central Amsterdam in the house he bought at the height of his success, has furnishings that are mostly not original, but period pieces comparable to those Rembrandt might have had, and paintings reflecting Rembrandt's use of the house for art dealing. His printmaking studio has been set up with a printing press, where replica prints are printed. The museum has a few Rembrandt paintings, many loaned, but an important collection of his prints, a good selection of which are on rotating display. All major <a href="/wiki/Print_room" title="Print room">print rooms</a> have large collections of Rembrandt prints, although as some exist in only a single impression, no collection is complete. The degree to which these collections are displayed to the public, or can easily be viewed by them in the print room, varies greatly.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_and_recognition">Influence and recognition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Influence and recognition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Old_master_print" title="Old master print">Old master print</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etching_Revival" title="Etching Revival">Etching Revival</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_Research_Project" title="Rembrandt Research Project">Rembrandt Research Project</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Rembrandt_van_Rijn" title="List of things named after Rembrandt van Rijn">List of things named after Rembrandt van Rijn</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_about_Rembrandt" title="List of works about Rembrandt">List of works about Rembrandt</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Rembrandt_pupils" title="List of Rembrandt pupils">List of Rembrandt pupils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Rembrandt_connoisseurs_and_scholars" title="List of Rembrandt connoisseurs and scholars">List of Rembrandt connoisseurs and scholars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg/220px-Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg/330px-Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg/440px-Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nachtwacht-in-3D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rembrandt statue and the sculptures of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i> in 3D at the <a href="/wiki/Rembrandtplein" title="Rembrandtplein">Rembrandtplein</a> in Amsterdam</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[...] I maintain that it did not occur to <a href="/wiki/Protogenes" title="Protogenes">Protogenes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apelles" title="Apelles">Apelles</a> or <a href="/wiki/Parrhasius_(painter)" title="Parrhasius (painter)">Parrhasius</a>, nor could it occur to them were they return to earth that (I am amazed simply to report this) a youth, a Dutchman, a beardless miller, could bring together so much in one human figure and express what is universal. All honor to thee, my Rembrandt! To have carried Illium, indeed all Asia, to Italy is a lesser achievement than to have brought the laurels of Greece and Italy to Holland, the achievement of a Dutchman who has seldom ventured outside the walls of his native city...</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Constantijn_Huygens" title="Constantijn Huygens">Constantijn Huygens</a>, Lord of Zuilichem, possibly the earliest known notable <a href="/wiki/List_of_Rembrandt_connoisseurs_and_scholars" title="List of Rembrandt connoisseurs and scholars">Rembrandt connoisseur and critic</a>, 1629. Excerpt from the manuscript <i>Autobiography of Constantijn Huygens</i> (Koninklijke Bibliotheek, Den Haag), originally published in <i>Oud Holland</i> (1891), translated from the Dutch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2941" data-file-width="1971" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg/170px-Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg/255px-Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg/340px-Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leiden_Rembrandt_Statue.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rembrandt statue in Leiden</div></div></div>
<p>Rembrandt is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Rembrandt_van_Rijn" title="List of things named after Rembrandt van Rijn">one of the most famous</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickey2006_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickey2006-113">[107]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_about_Rembrandt" title="List of works about Rembrandt">the best expertly researched</a> visual artists in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slive_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slive-114">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FranitsAshgate2016_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FranitsAshgate2016-115">[109]</a></sup> His life and art have long attracted the attention of interdisciplinary scholarship such as art history, socio-political history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup> cultural history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup> education, humanities, philosophy and aesthetics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup> psychology, sociology, literary studies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup> medicine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies" title="Religious studies">religious studies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[g]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[116]</a></sup> theology,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jewish_studies" title="Jewish studies">Jewish studies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oriental_studies" title="Oriental studies">Oriental studies</a> (<a href="/wiki/Asian_studies" title="Asian studies">Asian studies</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Global_studies" title="Global studies">global studies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[120]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Art_market" title="Art market">art market research</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[121]</a></sup> He has been the subject of a vast amount of literature <a href="/wiki/List_of_works_about_Rembrandt" title="List of works about Rembrandt">in genres of both fiction and nonfiction</a>. Research and scholarship related to Rembrandt is an academic field in its own right with many notable <a href="/wiki/List_of_Rembrandt_connoisseurs_and_scholars" title="List of Rembrandt connoisseurs and scholars">connoisseurs and scholars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup> and has been very dynamic since the Dutch Golden Age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slive_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Slive-114">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FranitsAshgate2016_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FranitsAshgate2016-115">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>
According to art historian and Rembrandt scholar Stephanie Dickey: </p><blockquote><p>[Rembrandt] earned international renown as a painter, printmaker, teacher, and art collector while never leaving the Dutch Republic. In his home city of Leiden and in Amsterdam, where he worked for nearly forty years, he mentored generations of other painters and produced a body of work that has never ceased to attract admiration, critique, and interpretation. (...) Rembrandt's art is a key component in any study of the Dutch Golden Age, and his membership in the canon of artistic genius is well established, but he is also a figure whose significance transcends specialist interest. Literary critics have pondered "Rembrandt" as a "cultural text"; novelists, playwrights, and filmmakers have romanticized his life, and in popular culture, his name has become synonymous with excellence for products and services, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_toothpaste" title="Rembrandt toothpaste">toothpaste</a> to self-help advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FranitsAshgate2016_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-FranitsAshgate2016-115">[109]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goethe_(Stieler_1828).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2432" data-file-width="1972" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Goethe_%28Stieler_1828%29.jpg/220px-Goethe_%28Stieler_1828%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Goethe_%28Stieler_1828%29.jpg/330px-Goethe_%28Stieler_1828%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Goethe_%28Stieler_1828%29.jpg/440px-Goethe_%28Stieler_1828%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goethe_(Stieler_1828).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1775, a 25-year-old <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a> wrote in a letter that "I live wholly with Rembrandt" ("...ich zeichne, künstle p. Und lebe ganz mit Rembrandt."). At the age of 81 (1831), Goethe wrote the essay "Rembrandt der Denker" ("Rembrandt the Thinker"), published in the posthumous collection of his works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[125]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Francisco_Goya" title="Francisco Goya">Francisco Goya</a>, often considered to be among the last of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Masters" title="Old Masters">Old Masters</a>, said, "I have had three masters: Nature, <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Velázquez</a>, and Rembrandt." ("Yo no he tenido otros maestros que la Naturaleza, Velázquez y Rembrandt.")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[128]</a></sup> In the history of the reception and interpretation of Rembrandt's art, it was the significant Rembrandt-inspired 'revivals' or 'rediscoveries' in 18th–19th century France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[130]</a></sup> Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup> and Britain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[137]</a></sup> that decisively helped in establishing his lasting fame in subsequent centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[138]</a></sup> When a critic referred to <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Rodin" title="Auguste Rodin">Auguste Rodin</a>'s busts in the same vein as Rembrandt's portraits, the French sculptor responded: "Compare me with Rembrandt? What sacrilege! With Rembrandt, the colossus of Art! What are you thinking of, my friend! We should prostrate ourselves before Rembrandt and never compare anyone with him!”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rodin_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rodin-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a> wrote to <a href="/wiki/Theo_van_Gogh_(art_dealer)" title="Theo van Gogh (art dealer)">his brother Theo</a> (1885), "Rembrandt goes so deep into the mysterious that he says things for which there are no words in any language. It is with justice that they call Rembrandt—magician—that's no easy occupation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rembrandt_and_the_Jewish_world">Rembrandt and the Jewish world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Rembrandt and the Jewish world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Amsterdam" title="History of the Jews in Amsterdam">History of the Jews in Amsterdam</a></div>
<p>Although Rembrandt was not Jewish, he has had a considerable influence on many modern Jewish artists, writers and scholars (<a href="/wiki/Art_critic" title="Art critic">art critics</a> and art historians in particular).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[141]</a></sup> The German-Jewish painter <a href="/wiki/Max_Liebermann" title="Max Liebermann">Max Liebermann</a> said, "Whenever I see a <a href="/wiki/Frans_Hals" title="Frans Hals">Frans Hals</a>, I feel like painting; whenever I see a Rembrandt, I feel like giving up."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marc_Chagall" title="Marc Chagall">Marc Chagall</a> wrote in 1922, "Neither <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" title="Imperial Russia">Imperial Russia</a>, nor the Russia of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviets" title="Soviets">Soviets</a> needs me. They don't understand me. I am a stranger to them," and he added, "I'm certain Rembrandt loves me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren,_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="5486" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren%2C_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren%2C_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren%2C_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren%2C_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren%2C_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren%2C_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_Portret_van_een_paar_als_oudtestamentische_figuren,_genaamd_%27Het_Joodse_bruidje%27_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Bride" title="The Jewish Bride">The Jewish Bride</a></i>, c. 1665–9, Rijksmuseum Amsterdam. In <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>'s own words (1885), "I should be happy to give 10 years of my life if I could go on sitting here in front of this picture [<i>The Jewish Bride</i>] fortnight, with only a crust of dry bread for food." In a letter to <a href="/wiki/Theo_van_Gogh_(art_dealer)" title="Theo van Gogh (art dealer)">his brother Theo</a>, Vincent wrote, "What an intimate, what an infinitely sympathetic picture it is,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[144]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It has proved a comfort to me, <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">in this era of European Jewish tragedy</a>, to dwell upon the life and work of Rembrandt. Here was a man of Germanic ancestry who did not regard the Jews in the Holland of his day as a "misfortune," but approached them with friendly sentiments, dwelt in their midst, and portrayed their personalities and ways of life. Rembrandt, moreover, regarded the Bible as the greatest Book in the world and held it in reverent affection all his life, in affluence and poverty, in success and failure. He never wearied in his devotion to biblical themes as subjects for his paintings and other graphic presentations, and in these portrayals he was the first to have the courage to use the Jews of his environment as models for the heroes of the sacred narratives.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Franz Landsberger, a German Jewish émigré to America, the author of <i>Rembrandt, the Jews, and the Bible</i> (1946)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[146]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_Rembrandt">Criticism of Rembrandt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Criticism of Rembrandt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Rembrandt has also been one of the most controversial (visual) artists in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slive_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-Slive-114">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Białostocki_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Białostocki-154">[147]</a></sup> Several of Rembrandt's notable critics include <a href="/wiki/Constantijn_Huygens" title="Constantijn Huygens">Constantijn Huygens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joachim_von_Sandrart" title="Joachim von Sandrart">Joachim von Sandrart</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivesRembrandt_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-LivesRembrandt-155">[148]</a></sup> <a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andries_Pels_(1631-1681)" title="nl:Andries Pels (1631-1681)">Andries Pels</a> (who called Rembrandt "the first heretic in the art of painting"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[149]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_van_Hoogstraten" title="Samuel van Hoogstraten">Samuel van Hoogstraten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Houbraken" title="Arnold Houbraken">Arnold Houbraken</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivesRembrandt_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-LivesRembrandt-155">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Baldinucci" title="Filippo Baldinucci">Filippo Baldinucci</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LivesRembrandt_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-LivesRembrandt-155">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gerard_de_Lairesse" title="Gerard de Lairesse">Gerard de Lairesse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_de_Piles" title="Roger de Piles">Roger de Piles</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruskin_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruskin-157">[150]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Fromentin" title="Eugène Fromentin">Eugène Fromentin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Białostocki_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Białostocki-154">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>By 1875 Rembrandt was already a powerful figure, projecting from historical past into the present with such a strength that he could not be simply overlooked or passed by. The great shadow of the old master required a decided attitude. A late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantic_painting" title="Romantic painting">Romantic painter</a> and critic, like <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Fromentin" title="Eugène Fromentin">Fromentin</a> was, if he happened not to like some of Rembrandt's pictures, he felt obliged to justify his feeling. The greatness of the dramatic old master was for artists of about 1875 not a matter for doubt. 'Either I am wrong', Fromentin wrote from Holland 'or everybody else is wrong'. When Fromentin realized his inability to like some of the works by Rembrandt he formulated the following comments: 'I even do not dare to write down such a blasphemy; I would get ridiculed if this is disclosed'. Only about twenty-five years earlier another French Romantic master <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a>, when expressing his admiration for Rembrandt, has written in his <i>Journal</i> a very different statement: '... perhaps one day we will discover that Rembrandt is a much greater painter than <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>. It is a blasphemy which would make hair raise on the heads of all the academic painters'. In 1851 the blasphemy was to put Rembrandt above Raphael. In 1875 the blasphemy was not to admire everything Rembrandt had ever produced. Between these two dates, the appreciation of Rembrandt reached its turning point and since that time he was never deprived of the high rank in the art world.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Rembrandt scholar <a href="/wiki/Jan_Bia%C5%82ostocki" title="Jan Białostocki">Jan Białostocki</a> (1972)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Białostocki_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-Białostocki-154">[147]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Cultural_depictions_of_Rembrandt" title="Category:Cultural depictions of Rembrandt">Category:Cultural depictions of Rembrandt</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[...] One thing that really surprises me is the extent to which Rembrandt exists as a phenomenon in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pop_culture" title="Pop culture">pop culture</a>. You have this <a href="/wiki/The_Rembrandts" title="The Rembrandts">musical group call [<i>sic</i>] the Rembrandts</a>, who wrote the theme song to <i>Friends</i>—"I'll Be There For You". There are Rembrandt restaurants, Rembrandt hotels, art supplies and other things that are more obvious. But then there's <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_toothpaste" title="Rembrandt toothpaste">Rembrandt toothpaste</a>. Why on Earth would somebody name a toothpaste after this artist who's known for his really dark tonalities? It doesn't make a lot of sense. But I think it's because his name has become synonymous with quality. It's even a verb—there's a term in underworld slang, 'to be Rembrandted,' which means to be framed for a crime. And people in the cinema world use it to mean pictorial effects that are overdone. He's just everywhere, and people who don't know anything, who wouldn't recognize a Rembrandt painting if they tripped over it, you say the name Rembrandt and they already know that this is a great artist. He's become a synonym for greatness.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Rembrandt scholar, Stephanie Dickey, in an interview with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Magazine" title="Smithsonian Magazine">Smithsonian Magazine</a></i>, December 2006<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickey2006_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dickey2006-113">[107]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>While shooting <i>The Warrens of Virginia</i> (1915), <a href="/wiki/Cecil_B._DeMille" title="Cecil B. DeMille">Cecil B. DeMille</a> had experimented with lighting instruments borrowed from a Los Angeles opera house. When business partner <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam_Goldwyn" title="Sam Goldwyn">Sam Goldwyn</a> saw a scene in which only half an actor's face was illuminated, he feared the exhibitors would pay only half the price for the picture. DeMille remonstrated that it was <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_lighting" title="Rembrandt lighting">Rembrandt lighting</a>. "Sam's reply was jubilant with relief," recalled DeMille. "For Rembrandt lighting the exhibitors would pay double!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_about_Rembrandt">Works about Rembrandt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Works about Rembrandt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Works_about_Rembrandt" title="Category:Works about Rembrandt">Category:Works about Rembrandt</a></div>

<li><i><a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rembrandt:_Fathers_%26_Sons" title="it:Rembrandt: Fathers &amp; Sons">Rembrandt: Fathers &amp; Sons</a></i> (1999 film directed by David Devine)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Stealing_Rembrandt" title="Stealing Rembrandt">Stealing Rembrandt</a></i> (2003 film directed by <a href="/wiki/Jannik_Johansen" title="Jannik Johansen">Jannik Johansen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anders_Thomas_Jensen" title="Anders Thomas Jensen">Anders Thomas Jensen</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Simon_Schama%27s_Power_of_Art" title="Simon Schama's Power of Art">Simon Schama's Power of Art</a>: Rembrandt</i> (2006 BBC documentary film series by Simon Schama)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Nightwatching" title="Nightwatching">Nightwatching</a></i> (2007 film directed by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Greenaway" title="Peter Greenaway">Peter Greenaway</a>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Rembrandt%27s_J%27Accuse" title="Rembrandt's J'Accuse">Rembrandt's J'Accuse</a></i> (2008 documentary film by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Greenaway" title="Peter Greenaway">Peter Greenaway</a>)</li>
<li><i><a class="extiw" href="https://nl.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rembrandt_en_ik" title="nl:Rembrandt en ik">Rembrandt en ik</a></i> (2011 film directed by Marleen Gorris)</li>
<li><i>Schama on Rembrandt: Masterpieces of the Late Years</i> (2014 documentary film by Simon Schama)</li>
<li><i>Rembrandt: From the National Gallery, London and Rijksmuseum, Amsterdam</i> (2014 documentary film by Exhibition on Screen)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Selected_works">Selected works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Selected works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_laughing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8171" data-file-width="6274" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Rembrandt_laughing.jpg/170px-Rembrandt_laughing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Rembrandt_laughing.jpg/255px-Rembrandt_laughing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Rembrandt_laughing.jpg/340px-Rembrandt_laughing.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_laughing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_Laughing" title="Rembrandt Laughing">Rembrandt Laughing</a></i>, 1628, <a href="/wiki/J._Paul_Getty_Museum" title="J. Paul Getty Museum">J. Paul Getty Museum</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2760" data-file-width="1992" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg/170px-Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg/255px-Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg/340px-Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame" title="The Girl in a Picture Frame">The Girl in a Picture Frame</a></i>, 1641, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Castle,_Warsaw" title="Royal Castle, Warsaw">Royal Castle, Warsaw</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2509" data-file-width="2024" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg/170px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg/255px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg/340px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_049.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The evangelist Matthew and the Angel</i>, 1661</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Stoning_of_Saint_Stephen" title="The Stoning of Saint Stephen">The Stoning of Saint Stephen</a></i> (1625) – <small><a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Fine_Arts_of_Lyon" title="Museum of Fine Arts of Lyon">Musée des Beaux-Arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Chained_to_the_Rocks" title="Andromeda Chained to the Rocks">Andromeda Chained to the Rocks</a></i> (1630) – <small><a href="/wiki/Mauritshuis" title="Mauritshuis">Mauritshuis</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Jacob_de_Gheyn_III" title="Jacob de Gheyn III">Jacob de Gheyn III</a></i> (1632) – <small><a href="/wiki/Dulwich_Picture_Gallery" title="Dulwich Picture Gallery">Dulwich Picture Gallery</a>, London</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Philosopher_in_Meditation" title="Philosopher in Meditation">Philosopher in Meditation</a></i> (1632) – <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Louvre" title="The Louvre">The Louvre</a>, Paris</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr._Nicolaes_Tulp" title="The Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp">The Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp</a></i> (1632) – <small><a href="/wiki/Mauritshuis" title="Mauritshuis">Mauritshuis</a>, The Hague</small></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemisia_(Rembrandt)" title="Artemisia (Rembrandt)">Artemisia</a></i> (1634) – <small>oil on canvas, 142 × 152 cm, <a href="/wiki/Museo_del_Prado" title="Museo del Prado">Museo del Prado</a>, Madrid</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Descent_from_the_Cross_(Rembrandt,_1634)" title="The Descent from the Cross (Rembrandt, 1634)">Descent from the Cross</a></i> (1634) – <small>oil on canvas, 158 × 117 cm, looted from the <a href="/wiki/Landgrave" title="Landgrave">Landgrave</a> of <a href="/wiki/Landgraviate_of_Hesse-Kassel" title="Landgraviate of Hesse-Kassel">Hesse-Kassel</a> (or Hesse-Cassel), Germany in 1806, currently <a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a>, St. Petersburg</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Belshazzar%27s_Feast_(Rembrandt)" title="Belshazzar's Feast (Rembrandt)">Belshazzar's Feast</a></i> (1635) – <small><a href="/wiki/National_Gallery" title="National Gallery">National Gallery</a>, London</small></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Prodigal_Son_in_the_Tavern" title="The Prodigal Son in the Tavern">The Prodigal Son in the Tavern</a></i> (c. 1635) – <small>oil on canvas, 161 × 131 cm <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie_Alte_Meister" title="Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister">Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dana%C3%AB_(Rembrandt_painting)" title="Danaë (Rembrandt painting)">Danaë</a></i> (1636 – c. 1643) – <small><a href="/wiki/Hermitage_Museum" title="Hermitage Museum">Hermitage Museum</a>, St. Petersburg</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Scholar_at_the_Lectern" title="The Scholar at the Lectern">The Scholar at the Lectern</a></i> (1641) – <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Castle_in_Warsaw" title="Royal Castle in Warsaw">Royal Castle in Warsaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Girl_in_a_Picture_Frame" title="The Girl in a Picture Frame">The Girl in a Picture Frame</a></i> (1641) – <small><a href="/wiki/Royal_Castle,_Warsaw" title="Royal Castle, Warsaw">Royal Castle, Warsaw</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i>, formally <i>The Militia Company of Captain Frans Banning Cocq</i> (1642) – <small><a href="/wiki/Rijksmuseum" title="Rijksmuseum">Rijksmuseum</a>, Amsterdam</small></li>
<li><i>Christ Healing the Sick</i> (<a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etching</a> c. 1643, also known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Hundred_Guilder_Print" title="Hundred Guilder Print">Hundred Guilder Print</a></i>)<small>, nicknamed for the huge sum paid for it</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Boaz_and_Ruth_(paintings)" title="Boaz and Ruth (paintings)">Boaz and Ruth</a></i> (1643) aka <i>The Old Rabbi</i> or <i>Old Man</i> – <small><a href="/wiki/Woburn_Abbey" title="Woburn Abbey">Woburn Abbey</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemaldegalerie,_Berlin" title="Gemaldegalerie, Berlin">Gemaldegalerie, Berlin</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Mill_(Rembrandt)" title="The Mill (Rembrandt)">The Mill</a></i> (1645/48) – <small><a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Art" title="National Gallery of Art">National Gallery of Art</a>, Washington, D.C.</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Old_Man_with_a_Gold_Chain" title="Old Man with a Gold Chain">Old Man with a Gold Chain</a></i> ("<i>Old Man with a Black Hat and Gorget</i>") (c. 1631) <small><a href="/wiki/Art_Institute_of_Chicago" title="Art Institute of Chicago">Art Institute of Chicago</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Susanna_and_the_Elders_(Rembrandt)" title="Susanna and the Elders (Rembrandt)">Susanna and the Elders</a></i> (1647) – <small>oil on panel, 76 × 91 cm, <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie,_Berlin" title="Gemäldegalerie, Berlin">Gemäldegalerie, Berlin</a></small></li>
<li><i>Head of Christ</i> (c. 1648–56) – <small>The Philadelphia Museum of Art</small><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotle_Contemplating_a_Bust_of_Homer" title="Aristotle Contemplating a Bust of Homer">Aristotle Contemplating a Bust of Homer</a></i> (1653) – <small><a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>, New York</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bathsheba_at_Her_Bath_(Rembrandt)" title="Bathsheba at Her Bath (Rembrandt)">Bathsheba at Her Bath</a></i> (1654) – <small>The Louvre, Paris</small></li>
<li><i>Christ Presented to the People (Ecce Homo)</i> (1655) – <small>Drypoint, <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_Museum_of_Art" title="Birmingham Museum of Art">Birmingham Museum of Art</a></small></li>
<li><i>Selfportrait</i> (1658) – <small><a href="/wiki/Frick_Collection" title="Frick Collection">Frick Collection</a>, New York</small></li>
<li><i>The Three Crosses</i> (1660) Etching, fourth state</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Ahasuerus_and_Haman_at_the_Feast_of_Esther" title="Ahasuerus and Haman at the Feast of Esther">Ahasuerus and Haman at the Feast of Esther</a></i> (1660) – <small><a href="/wiki/Pushkin_Museum" title="Pushkin Museum">Pushkin Museum</a>, Moscow</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Conspiracy_of_Claudius_Civilis" title="The Conspiracy of Claudius Civilis">The Conspiracy of Claudius Civilis</a></i> (1661) – <small><a href="/wiki/Nationalmuseum" title="Nationalmuseum">Nationalmuseum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Civilis" title="Gaius Julius Civilis">Claudius Civilis</a> led a Dutch revolt against the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>) (most of the cut up painting is lost, only the central part still exists)</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Portrait_of_Dirck_van_Os" title="Portrait of Dirck van Os">Portrait of Dirck van Os</a></i> (1662) – <small><a href="/wiki/Joslyn_Art_Museum" title="Joslyn Art Museum">Joslyn Art Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omaha,_Nebraska" title="Omaha, Nebraska">Omaha, Nebraska</a></small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Syndics_of_the_Drapers%27_Guild" title="Syndics of the Drapers' Guild">Syndics of the Drapers' Guild</a></i> (Dutch <i>De Staalmeesters</i>, 1662) – <small>Rijksmuseum, Amsterdam</small></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Bride" title="The Jewish Bride">The Jewish Bride</a></i> (1665) – <small>Rijksmuseum, Amsterdam</small></li>
<li><i>Haman before Esther</i> (1665) – <small><a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Art_of_Romania" title="National Museum of Art of Romania">National Museum of Art of Romania</a>, Bucharest</small> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>The Entombment Sketch</i> (c. 1639, reworked c. 1654) – <small>oil on oak panel, <a href="/wiki/Hunterian_Museum_and_Art_Gallery" title="Hunterian Museum and Art Gallery">Hunterian Museum and Art Gallery</a>, Glasgow</small></li>
<li><i>Saul and David</i> (c. 1660–1665) – <small><a href="/wiki/Mauritshuis" title="Mauritshuis">Mauritshuis</a>, The Hague</small></li>
<li><i>Portrait of an Old Man</i> (1645) – <a href="/wiki/Calouste_Gulbenkian_Museum" title="Calouste Gulbenkian Museum">Calouste Gulbenkian Museum</a>, Lisbon</li>
<li><i>Pallas Athena</i> (c.1657) – <a href="/wiki/Calouste_Gulbenkian_Museum" title="Calouste Gulbenkian Museum">Calouste Gulbenkian Museum</a>, Lisbon</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exhibitions">Exhibitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Exhibitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="703" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg/220px-Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg/330px-Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg/440px-Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moving_Rembrandt%27s_%27Nightwatch%27.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Moving Rembrandt's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i> for the 1898 Rembrandt Exhibition</div></div></div>
<ul><li>Sept–Oct 1898: <i>Rembrandt Tentoonstelling</i> (<i>Rembrandt Exhibition</i>), Stedelijk Museum, Amsterdam, The Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rembrandt_tentoonstilling_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rembrandt_tentoonstilling-161">[154]</a></sup></li>
<li>Jan–Feb 1899: <i>Rembrandt Tentoonstelling</i> (<i>Rembrandt Exhibition</i>), Royal Academy, London, England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rembrandt_tentoonstilling_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rembrandt_tentoonstilling-161">[154]</a></sup></li>
<li>21 April 2011 – 18 July 2011: <i>Rembrandt and the Face of Jesus</i>, Musée du Louvre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li>16 September 2013 – 14 November 2013: <i>Rembrandt: The Consummate Etcher</i>, Syracuse University Art Galleries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[156]</a></sup></li>
<li>19 May 2014 – 27 June 2014: <i>From Rembrandt to Rosenquist: Works on Paper from the NAC's Permanent Collection</i>, National Arts Club.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li>19 October 2014 – 4 January 2015: <i>Rembrandt, Rubens, Gainsborough and the Golden Age of Painting in Europe</i>, Jule Collins Smith Museum of Art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[158]</a></sup></li>
<li>15 October 2014 – 18 January 2015: <i>Rembrandt: The Late Works</i>, The National Gallery, London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[159]</a></sup></li>
<li>12 February 2015 – 17 May 2015: <i>Late Rembrandt</i>, The Rijksmuseum, Amsterdam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[160]</a></sup></li>
<li>16 September 2018 – 6 January 2019: <i>Rembrandt – Painter as Printmaker</i>, Denver Art Museum, Denver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[161]</a></sup></li>
<li>24 Aug 2019 – 1 December 2019: <i>Leiden circa 1630: Rembrandt Emerges</i>, Agnes Etherington Art Centre, Kingston, Ontario<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li>4 October 2019 – 2 February 2020: <i>Rembrandt's Light</i>, Dulwich Picture Gallery, London<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[163]</a></sup></li>
<li>18 February 2020 – 30 August 2020: <i>Rembrandt and Amsterdam portraiture, 1590–1670 </i>, Museo Nacional Thyssen-Bornemisza, Madrid<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thyssen_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thyssen-171">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>10 August 2020 – 1 November 2020: <i>Young Rembrandt</i>, Ashmolean Museum, Oxford<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[165]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paintings">Paintings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Paintings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-alpha" style="column-width: 30em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This version of his first name, "Rembrandt" with a "d," first appeared in his signatures in 1633. Until then, he had signed with a combination of initials or monograms. In late 1632, he began signing solely with his first name, "Rembrant". He added the "d" in the following year and stuck to this spelling for the rest of his life. Although scholars can only speculate, this change must have had a meaning for Rembrandt, which is generally interpreted as his wanting to be known by his first name like the great figures of the Italian Renaissance: Leonardo, Raphael etc., who did not sign with their last names, if at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Useful totals of the figures from various different oeuvre catalogues, often divided into classes along the lines of: "very likely authentic", "possibly authentic" and "unlikely to be authentic" are given at <a class="external text" href="http://staff.science.uva.nl/~fjseins/RembrandtCatalogue/" rel="nofollow">the Online Rembrandt catalogue</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Two hundred years ago Bartsch listed 375. More recent catalogues have added three (two in unique impressions) and excluded enough to reach totals as follows: Schwartz, p. 6, 289; Münz 1952, 279; Boon 1963, 287 <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20000831232904/http://www.printcouncil.org/search.html" rel="nofollow">Print Council of America</a> – but Schwartz's total quoted does not tally with the book.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is not possible to give a total, as a new wave of scholarship on Rembrandt drawings is still in progress – analysis of the Berlin collection for an exhibition in 2006/7 has produced a probable drop from 130 sheets there to about 60. <a class="external text" href="http://www.codart.nl/exhibitions/details/911/" rel="nofollow">Codart.nl</a> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[42]</a></sup> The British Museum is due to publish a new catalogue after a similar exercise.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While the popular interpretation is that these paintings represent a personal and introspective journey, it is possible that they were painted to satisfy a market for self-portraits by prominent artists. Van de Wetering, p. 290.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Such as <a href="/wiki/Otto_Benesch" title="Otto Benesch">Otto Benesch</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Hockney" title="David Hockney">David Hockney</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hockney2014_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hockney2014-78">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Konstam" title="Nigel Konstam">Nigel Konstam</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jakob_Rosenberg_(art_historian)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jakob Rosenberg (art historian) (page does not exist)">Jakob Rosenberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gary_Schwartz_(art_historian)" title="Gary Schwartz (art historian)">Gary Schwartz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Slive" title="Seymour Slive">Seymour Slive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It is important to note that Rembrandt's religious affiliation was uncertain. And there is no evidence that Rembrandt formally belonged to any <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denomination</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In Rembrandt's (late) great portraits we feel face to face with real people, we sense their warmth, their need for sympathy and also their loneliness and suffering. Those keen and steady eyes that we know so well from Rembrandt's self-portraits must have been able to look straight into the human heart." Gombrich, p. 423.</span>

<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It (<i>The Jewish Bride</i>) is a picture of grown-up love, a marvelous amalgam of richness, tenderness, and trust... the heads which, in their truth, have a spiritual glow that painters influenced by the classical tradition could never achieve." Clark, p. 206.</span>
