<li id="cite_note-Hockney2014-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Hockney2014_78-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Hockney2014_78-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFLewis,_Tim2014">Lewis, Tim (16 November 2014). <a class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2014/nov/16/david-hockney-interview-i-feel-like-picasso" rel="nofollow">"David Hockney: 'When I'm working, I feel like Picasso, I feel I'm 30'<span class="cs1-kern-right"></span>"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200516011950/https://www.theguardian.com/artanddesign/2014/nov/16/david-hockney-interview-i-feel-like-picasso" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 16 May 2020<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">16 June</span> 2020</span>. <q><a href="/wiki/David_Hockney" title="David Hockney">David Hockney</a> (2014): "There's a drawing by Rembrandt, I think it's the greatest drawing ever done. It's in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> and it's of a family teaching a child to walk, so it's a universal thing, everybody has experienced this or seen it happen. Everybody. I used to print out Rembrandt drawings big and give them to people and say: 'If you find a better drawing send it to me. But if you find a better one it will be by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goya" title="Goya">Goya</a> or <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> perhaps.' But I don't think there is one actually. It's a magnificent drawing, magnificent."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Guardian&amp;rft.atitle=David+Hockney%3A+%27When+I%27m+working%2C+I+feel+like+Picasso%2C+I+feel+I%27m+30%27&amp;rft.date=2014-11-16&amp;rft.au=Lewis%2C+Tim&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.theguardian.com%2Fartanddesign%2F2014%2Fnov%2F16%2Fdavid-hockney-interview-i-feel-like-picasso&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARembrandt"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Mendelowitz-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Mendelowitz_82-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Mendelowitz_82-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Mendelowitz, Daniel Marcus: <i>Drawing</i>. (New York: Holt, Rinehart &amp; Winston, Inc., 1967), p. 305. As Mendelowitz (1967) noted: "Probably no one has combined to as great a degree as Rembrandt a disciplined exposition of what his eye saw and a love of line as a beautiful thing in itself. His "Winter Landscape" displays the virtuosity of performance of an Oriental master, yet unlike the Oriental calligraphy, it is not based on an established convention of brush performance. It is as personal as handwriting."</span>

<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">*<a href="/wiki/Antonio_Negri" title="Antonio Negri">Negri, Antonio</a>: <i>The Savage Anomaly: The Power of Spinoza's Metaphysics and Politics</i>. (Minneapolis: University of Minnesota Press, 1991). Translated from the Italian by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hardt" title="Michael Hardt">Michael Hardt</a>. Originally published as <i>L'anomalia selvaggia. Saggio su potere e potenza in Baruch Spinoza</i> (Milano: Feltrinelli, 1981). <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Negri" title="Antonio Negri">Antonio Negri</a> (1981): "<a href="/wiki/Leszek_Ko%C5%82akowski" title="Leszek Kołakowski">Kolakowski</a>, as we will see, has clearly depicted the religious life and the forms of community constructed by the cultured strata of the Dutch bourgeoisie. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedictus_de_Spinoza" title="Benedictus de Spinoza">Spinoza</a> lives in this world, with a vast network of simple and sociable friendships and correspondences. But for certain determinate strata of the bourgeoisie the sweetness of the cultured and sedate life is accompanied, without any contradiction, by an association with a capitalist power ('potestas'), expressed in very mature terms. This is the condition of a Dutch bourgeois man. We could say the same thing for the other genius of that age, Rembrandt van Rijn. On his canvases the power of light is concentrated with intensity on the figures of a bourgeois world in terrific expansion. It is a prosaic but very powerful society, which makes poetry without knowing it because it has the force to do so."
<ul><li>Ahmad, Iftikhar (2008), 'Art in Social Studies: Exploring the World and Ourselves with Rembrandt,'. <i>The Journal of Aesthetic Education</i> 42(2): 19–37</li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFMolyneux,_John2019"><a href="/wiki/John_Molyneux_(academic)" title="John Molyneux (academic)">Molyneux, John</a> (5 July 2019). <a class="external text" href="http://www.rebelnews.ie/2019/07/05/the-dialectics-of-art/" rel="nofollow">"The Dialectics of Art"</a>. Rebelnews.ie. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200630082351/http://www.rebelnews.ie/2019/07/05/the-dialectics-of-art/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 30 June 2020<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">25 June</span> 2020</span>. <q><a href="/wiki/John_Molyneux_(academic)" title="John Molyneux (academic)">John Molyneux</a> (2019): "...In short there is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialectical" title="Dialectical">dialectical</a> relationship between art and society. Thus two major artists from the same period and the same part of the world may respond to the same historical events in very different ways. One example would be <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Rubens</a> and Rembrandt. Rubens (1577–1640) was based in Antwerp and Rembrandt (1606–1669) in Amsterdam. Rembrandt's art was very much a product of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_Revolt" title="Dutch Revolt">Dutch Revolt</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bourgeois_revolution" title="Bourgeois revolution">bourgeois revolution</a> which established the Dutch Republic, while Rubens' art was commissioned by and expressed the aristocratic counter-revolution of the Habsburg Empire."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Dialectics+of+Art&amp;rft.pub=Rebelnews.ie&amp;rft.date=2019-07-05&amp;rft.au=Molyneux%2C+John&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.rebelnews.ie%2F2019%2F07%2F05%2Fthe-dialectics-of-art%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARembrandt"></span></li></ul>
